      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_culture">
        Popular culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Popular culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Romanian has become popular in other countries through movies and songs performed in the Romanian language. Examples of Romanian acts that had a great success in non-Romanophone countries are the bands
       <a href="/wiki/O-Zone" title="O-Zone">
        O-Zone
       </a>
       (with their No. 1 single
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dragostea_Din_Tei" title="Dragostea Din Tei">
         Dragostea Din Tei
        </a>
        /Numa Numa
       </i>
       across the world in 2003–2004),
       <a href="/wiki/Akcent" title="Akcent">
        Akcent
       </a>
       (popular in the Netherlands, Poland and other European countries),
       <a href="/wiki/Activ_(band)" title="Activ (band)">
        Activ
       </a>
       (successful in some Eastern European countries),
       <a href="/wiki/DJ_Project" title="DJ Project">
        DJ Project
       </a>
       (popular as clubbing music)
       <a href="/wiki/SunStroke_Project" title="SunStroke Project">
        SunStroke Project
       </a>
       (known by viral video "Epic sax guy") and
       <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Stan" title="Alexandra Stan">
        Alexandra Stan
       </a>
       (worldwide no.1 hit with "
       <a href="/wiki/Mr._Saxobeat" title="Mr. Saxobeat">
        Mr. Saxobeat
       </a>
       )" and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inna_(singer)" title="Inna (singer)">
        Inna
       </a>
       as well as high-rated movies like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/4_Months,_3_Weeks_and_2_Days" title="4 Months, 3 Weeks and 2 Days">
         4 Months, 3 Weeks and 2 Days
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_Mr._Lazarescu" title="The Death of Mr. Lazarescu">
         The Death of Mr. Lazarescu
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/12:08_East_of_Bucharest" title="12:08 East of Bucharest">
         12:08 East of Bucharest
        </a>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/California_Dreamin%27_(film)" title="California Dreamin' (film)">
         California Dreamin'
        </a>
       </i>
       (all of them with awards at the
       <a href="/wiki/Cannes_Film_Festival" title="Cannes Film Festival">
        Cannes Film Festival
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Also some artists wrote songs dedicated to the Romanian language. The multiplatinum pop trio
       <a href="/wiki/O-Zone" title="O-Zone">
        O-Zone
       </a>
       (originally from Moldova) released a song called "
       <i>
        Nu mă las de limba noastră
       </i>
       " ("I won't forsake our language"). The final verse of this song,
       <i>
        Eu nu mă las de limba noastră, de limba noastră cea română
       </i>
       is translated in English as "I won't forsake our language, our Romanian language". Also, the Moldovan musicians
       <a href="/wiki/Doina_and_Ion_Aldea_Teodorovici" title="Doina and Ion Aldea Teodorovici">
        Doina and Ion Aldea Teodorovici
       </a>
       performed a song called "The Romanian language".
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">
        Dialects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dialects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_dialects" title="Romanian dialects">
        Romanian dialects
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The term "Romanian" is sometimes
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       used also in a more general sense, encompassing four varieties: (Daco-)Romanian,
       <a href="/wiki/Aromanian_language" title="Aromanian language">
        Aromanian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Megleno-Romanian_language" title="Megleno-Romanian language">
        Megleno-Romanian
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Istro-Romanian_language" title="Istro-Romanian language">
        Istro-Romanian
       </a>
       . The four languages, whose mutual intelligibility is low, are the offspring of the
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance varieties
       </a>
       spoken both to the north and to south of the
       <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">
        Danube
       </a>
       , before the settlement of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" title="Slavic peoples">
        Slavonian
       </a>
       tribes south of the river: Daco-Romanian in the north, Aromanian and Megleno-Romanian in the south, whereas Istro-Romanian is thought to be the offspring of an 11th-century migration from Romania. These four are also known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Romance_languages" title="Eastern Romance languages">
        Eastern Romance languages
       </a>
       . When the term "Romanian" is used in this larger sense, the term "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daco-Romanian" title="Daco-Romanian">
        Daco-Romanian
       </a>
       " is used for Romanian proper. The origin of the term "Daco-Romanian" can be traced back to the first printed book of Romanian grammar in 1780,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elementa_32-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Elementa-32">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuil_Micu" title="Samuil Micu">
        Samuil Micu
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Gheorghe_%C8%98incai" title="Gheorghe Șincai">
        Gheorghe Șincai
       </a>
       . There, the Romanian dialect spoken north of the
       <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">
        Danube
       </a>
       is called
       <i>
        lingua Daco-Romana
       </i>
       to emphasize its origin and its area of use, which includes the former
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">
        Roman
       </a>
       province of
       <a href="/wiki/Dacia" title="Dacia">
        Dacia
       </a>
       , although it is spoken also south of the Danube, in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dobrudja" title="Dobrudja">
        Dobrudja
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vlachs_of_Serbia" title="Vlachs of Serbia">
        Central Serbia
       </a>
       and northern Bulgaria.
      </p>
      <p>
       This article deals with the Romanian (i.e. Daco-Romanian) language, and thus only its dialectal variations are discussed here. The differences between the regional varieties are small, limited to regular phonetic changes, few grammar aspects, and lexical particularities. There is a single written standard (literary) Romanian language used by all speakers, regardless of region. Like most natural languages, Romanian dialects are part of a
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">
        dialect continuum
       </a>
       . The dialects of Romanian are also referred to as
       <i>
        subdialects
       </i>
       (see
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_dialects#Terminology" title="Romanian dialects">
        reasons for this terminology
       </a>
       ) and are distinguished primarily by phonetic differences. Romanians themselves speak of the differences as
       <i>
        accents
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        speeches
       </i>
       (in Romanian:
       <i>
        accent
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        grai
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Depending on the criteria used for classifying these dialects, fewer or more are found, ranging from 2 to 20, although the most widespread approaches give a number of five dialects. These are grouped into two main types, southern and northern, further divided as follows:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The southern type has only one member:
        <ul>
         <li>
          the
          <a href="/wiki/Wallachian_dialect" title="Wallachian dialect">
           Wallachian dialect
          </a>
          , spoken in the southern part of Romania, in the historical regions of
          <a href="/wiki/Muntenia" title="Muntenia">
           Muntenia
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Oltenia" title="Oltenia">
           Oltenia
          </a>
          and the southern part of
          <a href="/wiki/Northern_Dobruja" title="Northern Dobruja">
           Northern Dobruja
          </a>
          , but also extending in the southern parts of
          <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">
           Transylvania
          </a>
          .
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        The northern type consists of several dialects:
        <ul>
         <li>
          the
          <a href="/wiki/Moldavian_dialect" title="Moldavian dialect">
           Moldavian dialect
          </a>
          , spoken in the historical region of
          <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">
           Moldavia
          </a>
          , now split among Romania, the Republic of Moldova, and Ukraine (
          <a href="/wiki/Bukovina" title="Bukovina">
           Bukovina
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/Bessarabia" title="Bessarabia">
           Bessarabia
          </a>
          ), as well as northern part of
          <a href="/wiki/Northern_Dobruja" title="Northern Dobruja">
           Northern Dobruja
          </a>
          ;
         </li>
         <li>
          the
          <a href="/wiki/Banat_Romanian_dialect" title="Banat Romanian dialect">
           Banat dialect
          </a>
          , spoken in the historical region of
          <a href="/wiki/Banat" title="Banat">
           Banat
          </a>
          , including parts of Serbia;
         </li>
         <li>
          a group of finely divided and transition-like
          <a href="/wiki/Transylvanian_varieties_of_Romanian" title="Transylvanian varieties of Romanian">
           Transylvanian varieties
          </a>
          , among which two are most often distinguished, those of
          <a href="/wiki/Cri%C8%99ana_dialect" title="Crișana dialect">
           Crișana
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/Maramure%C8%99_dialect" title="Maramureș dialect">
           Maramureș
          </a>
          .
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Over the last century, however, regional accents have been weakened due to mass communication and greater mobility.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Romance_languages_and_Romanian.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="951" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Romance_languages_and_Romanian.png/450px-Romance_languages_and_Romanian.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Romance_languages_and_Romanian.png/675px-Romance_languages_and_Romanian.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Romance_languages_and_Romanian.png/900px-Romance_languages_and_Romanian.png 2x" width="450"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Romance_languages_and_Romanian.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Romanian language in the Romance language family
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Romanian is a Romance language, belonging to the
       <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">
        Italic branch
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European language family
       </a>
       , having much in common with languages such as French, Italian, Spanish and Portuguese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoica1_83-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stoica1-83">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       However, the languages closest to Romanian are the other
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Romance_languages" title="Eastern Romance languages">
        Eastern Romance languages
       </a>
       , spoken south of the Danube:
       <a href="/wiki/Aromanian_language" title="Aromanian language">
        Aromanian/Macedo-Romanian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Megleno-Romanian_language" title="Megleno-Romanian language">
        Megleno-Romanian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Istro-Romanian_language" title="Istro-Romanian language">
        Istro-Romanian
       </a>
       , which are frequently classified as dialects of Romanian. An alternative name for Romanian used by linguists to disambiguate with the other Eastern Romance languages is "Daco-Romanian", referring to the area where it is spoken (which corresponds roughly to the onetime
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman
       </a>
       province of
       <a href="/wiki/Dacia" title="Dacia">
        Dacia
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Compared with the other Romance languages, the closest relative of Romanian is Italian;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoica1_83-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stoica1-83">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the two languages show a limited degree of asymmetrical
       <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">
        mutual intelligibility
       </a>
       , especially in their cultivated forms: speakers of Romanian seem to understand Italian more easily than the other way around.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2007)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Romanian has obvious grammatical and
       <a href="/wiki/Lexical_similarity" title="Lexical similarity">
        lexical similarities
       </a>
       with French,
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       , Spanish and Portuguese, with a high phonological similarity with Portuguese in particular; however, it is not mutually intelligible with them to any practical extent.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="can we add a source please? (November 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Romanian speakers will usually need some formal study of basic grammar and vocabulary before being able to understand more than individual words and simple sentences in other Romance languages.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="can we add a source please? (November 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The same is true for speakers of these languages trying to understand Romanian.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="can we add a source please? (November 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Because of its separation from the other Romance languages, it has diverged from them and is an outlier in various ways, somewhat like English in regards to the other Germanic languages.
      </p>
      <p>
       Romanian has had a greater share of foreign influence than some other Romance languages such as Italian in terms of vocabulary and other aspects. One such study was done by Italian-American linguist
       <a href="/wiki/Mario_Pei" title="Mario Pei">
        Mario Pei
       </a>
       in 1949, which analyzed the differentiation degree of languages in comparison to their inheritance language (in the case of
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       comparing
       <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">
        phonology
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">
        inflection
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">
        discourse
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">
        syntax
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">
        vocabulary
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">
        intonation
       </a>
       ) revealed the following percentages (the higher the percentage, the greater the distance from Latin):
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">
         Sardinian
        </a>
        : 8%;
       </li>
       <li>
        Italian: 12%;
       </li>
       <li>
        Spanish: 20%;
       </li>
       <li>
        Romanian: 23.5%;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
         Occitan
        </a>
        : 25%;
       </li>
       <li>
        Portuguese: 31%;
       </li>
       <li>
        French: 44%.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Lexical_similarity" title="Lexical similarity">
        lexical similarity
       </a>
       of Romanian with Italian has been estimated at 77%, followed by French at 75%, Sardinian 74%, Catalan 73%, Portuguese and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhaeto-Romance" title="Rhaeto-Romance">
        Rhaeto-Romance
       </a>
       72%, Spanish 71%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Romanian vocabulary became predominantly influenced by French and, to a lesser extent, Italian in the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dacian_language">
        Dacian language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Dacian language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Dacian_language" title="Dacian language">
        Dacian language
       </a>
       was an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_language" title="Indo-European language">
        Indo-European language
       </a>
       spoken by the ancient Dacians, mostly north of the Danube river but also in
       <a href="/wiki/Moesia" title="Moesia">
        Moesia
       </a>
       and other regions south of the Danube. It may have been the first language to influence the Latin spoken in Dacia, but little is known about it. Dacian is usually considered to have been a northern branch of the
       <a href="/wiki/Thracian_language" title="Thracian language">
        Thracian language
       </a>
       , and, like Thracian, Dacian was a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satem_language" title="Satem language">
        satem language
       </a>
       . About 300 words found only in Romanian or with a cognate in the
       <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">
        Albanian language
       </a>
       may be inherited from Dacian (for example:
       <i>
        barză
       </i>
       "
       <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">
        stork
       </a>
       ",
       <i>
        balaur
       </i>
       "dragon",
       <i>
        mal
       </i>
       "shore",
       <i>
        brânză
       </i>
       "cheese").
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Some of these possibly Dacian words are related to pastoral life (for example,
       <i>
        brânză
       </i>
       "cheese"). Some linguists and historians have asserted that Albanians are Dacians who were not Romanized and migrated southward.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-georgiev_87-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-georgiev-87">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A different view is that these non-Latin words with Albanian
       <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">
        cognates
       </a>
       are not necessarily Dacian, but rather were brought into the territory that is modern Romania by Romance-speaking shepherds migrating north from Albania, Serbia, and northern Greece who became the Romanian people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Balkan_language_area">
        Balkan language area
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Balkan language area">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       While most of Romanian grammar and morphology are based on Latin, there are some features that are shared only with other languages of the Balkans and not found in other Romance languages. The shared features of Romanian and the other languages of the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkan_sprachbund" title="Balkan sprachbund">
        Balkan language area
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">
        Bulgarian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_language" title="Macedonian language">
        Macedonian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">
        Albanian
       </a>
       , Greek, and
       <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">
        Serbian
       </a>
       ) include a suffixed
       <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">
        definite article
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Syncretism_(linguistics)" title="Syncretism (linguistics)">
        syncretism
       </a>
       of genitive and dative case and the formation of the future and the alternation of infinitive with subjunctive constructions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to a well-established scholarly theory, most Balkanisms could be traced back to the development of the Balkan Romance languages; these features were adopted by other languages due to
       <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">
        language shift
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Slavic_languages">
        Slavic languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Slavic languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Slavic_influence_on_Romanian" title="Slavic influence on Romanian">
        Slavic influence on Romanian
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Slavic influences on Romanian are especially noticeable and can be observed at all linguistic levels: lexis, phonetics, morphology and syntax.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       About 20% of modern Romanian words are presently of Slavic origin, but this number was significantly higher before Romanian was re-Latinized.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Slavic tribes lived in the territory of present-day Romania during the early evolution of the language and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic" title="Church Slavonic">
        Church Slavonic
       </a>
       was used continuously as the
       <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">
        liturgical language
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Romanian Orthodox Church">
        Romanian Orthodox Church
       </a>
       . This process of the introduction of Slavic in Dacia was similar to the appearance of various Germanic dialects in the Western Roman Empire, where Gallic Latin, Iberian Latin, and Northern Italian dialects became strongly Germanized.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       However, due to the lower Romance-speaking populace in the East, Slavic remained spoken for much longer and did not die out immediately.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_influences">
        Other influences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other influences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Even before the 19th century, Romanian came in contact with several other languages. Some notable examples include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Greek:
        <i>
         folos
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         ófelos
        </i>
        "use",
        <i>
         buzunar
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         buzunára
        </i>
        "pocket",
        <i>
         proaspăt
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         prósfatos
        </i>
        "fresh",
        <i>
         cutie
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         cution
        </i>
        "box",
        <i>
         portocale
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         portokalia
        </i>
        "oranges". While Latin borrowed words of Greek origin, Romanian obtained Greek loanwords on its own. Greek entered Romanian through the
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apoikia" title="Apoikia">
          apoikiai
         </a>
        </i>
        (colonies) and
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Emporium_(antiquity)" title="Emporium (antiquity)">
          emporia
         </a>
        </i>
        (trade stations) founded in and around
        <a href="/wiki/Dobruja" title="Dobruja">
         Dobruja
        </a>
        , through the presence of
        <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
         Byzantine Empire
        </a>
        in north of the
        <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">
         Danube
        </a>
        , through
        <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">
         Bulgarian
        </a>
        during Bulgarian Empires that converted Romanians to Orthodox Christianity, and after the Greek Civil War, when thousands of Greeks fled Greece.
       </li>
       <li>
        Hungarian:
        <i>
         a cheltui
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         költeni
        </i>
        "to spend",
        <i>
         a făgădui
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         fogadni
        </i>
        "to promise",
        <i>
         a mântui
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         menteni
        </i>
        "to save",
        <i>
         oraș
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         város
        </i>
        "city";
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">
         Turkish
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         papuc
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         pabuç
        </i>
        "slipper",
        <i>
         ciorbă
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         çorba
        </i>
        "wholemeal soup, sour soup",
        <i>
         bacșiș
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         bahşiş
        </i>
        "tip" (ultimately from Persian
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Baksheesh" title="Baksheesh">
          baksheesh
         </a>
        </i>
        );
       </li>
       <li>
        German:
        <i>
         cartof
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Kartoffel
        </i>
        "potato",
        <i>
         bere
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Bier
        </i>
        "beer",
        <i>
         șurub
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Schraube
        </i>
        "screw",
        <i>
         turn
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Turm
        </i>
        "tower",
        <i>
         ramă
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Rahmen
        </i>
        "frame",
        <i>
         muștiuc
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Mundstück
        </i>
        "mouth piece",
        <i>
         bormașină
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Bohrmaschine
        </i>
        "drilling machine",
        <i>
         cremșnit
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Kremschnitte
        </i>
        "cream slice",
        <i>
         șvaițer
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Schweizer
        </i>
        "Swiss cheese",
        <i>
         șlep
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Schleppkahn
        </i>
        "barge",
        <i>
         șpriț
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Spritzer
        </i>
        "wine with soda water",
        <i>
         abțibild
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Abziehbild
        </i>
        "decal picture",
        <i>
         șnițel
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Schnitzel
        </i>
        "cutlet",
        <i>
         șuncă
        </i>
        &lt; dialectal
        <i>
         Schunke
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         Schinken
        </i>
        ) "ham",
        <i>
         punct
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Punkt
        </i>
        "point",
        <i>
         maistru
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Meister
        </i>
        "master",
        <i>
         rundă
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Runde
        </i>
        "round". During the Austrian administration in
        <a href="/wiki/Banat" title="Banat">
         Banat
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">
         Transylvania
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Bukovina" title="Bukovina">
         Bukovina
        </a>
        , a large number of words were borrowed from Austrian German, in particular in fields such as the military, administration, social welfare, economy, etc.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
         <a href="#cite_note-92">
          [91]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Later on German terms have been taken out of science and technics, like:
        <i>
         șină
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Schiene
        </i>
        "rail",
        <i>
         știft
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Stift
        </i>
        "peg",
        <i>
         liță
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Litze
        </i>
        "braid",
        <i>
         șindrilă
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Schindel
        </i>
        "shingle",
        <i>
         ștanță
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Stanze
        </i>
        "punch",
        <i>
         șaibă
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Scheibe
        </i>
        "washer",
        <i>
         ștangă
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Stange
        </i>
        "crossbar",
        <i>
         țiglă
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Ziegel
        </i>
        "tile",
        <i>
         șmirghel
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         Schmirgelpapier
        </i>
        "emery paper";
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Romani_language" title="Romani language">
         Romani language
        </a>
        has provided a series of words to Romanian, especially slang terms:
        <i>
         mișto
        </i>
        "good, beautiful, cool" &lt;
        <i>
         mišto
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
         <a href="#cite_note-93">
          [92]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <i>
         gagică
        </i>
        "girlie, girlfriend" &lt;
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Gadjo_(non-Romani)" title="Gadjo (non-Romani)">
          gadji
         </a>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         a hali
        </i>
        "to devour" &lt;
        <i>
         halo
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         mandea
        </i>
        "yours truly" &lt;
        <i>
         mande
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         a mangli
        </i>
        "to pilfer" &lt;
        <i>
         manglo
        </i>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In addition, many more words were borrowed from
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        Classical Latin
       </a>
       through the influence of written language and the liturgical language of the Roman Catholic Church, as Latin did hold an important position in
       <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">
        Transylvania
       </a>
       after the 11th century as it was part of Kingdom of Hungary, a Roman Catholic country, until it was occupied by Romanians after World War I.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Throughout the Middle Ages and into the early modern period, most literate Romanian speakers, including Roman Catholics and Orthodox, were also literate in Latin; and thus they easily adopted Latin words, along with Hungarian, into their writing—and eventually speech—in Romanian. This caused Romanian to easily reduce some of its Slavic loanwords.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="French.2C_Italian_and_English_words">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="French,_Italian_and_English_words">
        French, Italian and English words
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: French, Italian and English words">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Since the 19th century, many literary or learned words were borrowed from the other Romance languages, especially from French and Italian (for example:
       <i>
        birou
       </i>
       "desk, office",
       <i>
        avion
       </i>
       "airplane",
       <i>
        exploata
       </i>
       "exploit"). It was estimated that about 38% of words in Romanian are of French and/or Italian origin (in many cases both languages); and adding this to Romanian's native stock, about 75%–85% of Romanian words can be traced to Latin. The use of these Romanianized French and Italian learned loans has tended to increase at the expense of Slavic loanwords, many of which have become rare or fallen out of use. As second or third languages, French and Italian themselves are better known in Romania than in Romania's neighbors. Along with the switch to the Latin alphabet in Moldova, the re-latinization of the vocabulary has tended to reinforce the Latin character of the language.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the process of lexical modernization, much of the native Latin stock have acquired doublets from other
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       , thus forming a further and more modern and literary lexical layer. Typically, the native word is a noun and the learned loan is an adjective. Some examples of doublets:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
       <tr>
        <th>

        </th>

          </span>

         </i>

        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       In the 20th century, an increasing number of English words have been borrowed (such as:
       <i>
        gem
       </i>
       &lt; jam;
       <i>
        interviu
       </i>
       &lt; interview;
       <i>
        meci
       </i>
       &lt; match;
       <i>
        manager
       </i>
       &lt; manager;
       <i>
        fotbal
       </i>
       &lt; football;
       <i>
        sandviș
       </i>
       &lt; sandwich;
       <i>
        bișniță
       </i>
       &lt; business;
       <i>
        chec
       </i>
       &lt; cake). These words are assigned grammatical gender in Romanian and handled according to Romanian rules; thus "the manager" is
       <i>
        managerul
       </i>
       . Some borrowings, for example in the computer field, appear to have awkward (perhaps contrived and ludicrous) 'Romanisation,' such as
       <i>
        cookie-uri
       </i>
       which is the plural of the Internet term
       <i>
        cookie.
       </i>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lexis">
        Lexis
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Lexis">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CuvinteleLimbiiRomane.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="694" data-file-width="549" height="379" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/CuvinteleLimbiiRomane.svg/300px-CuvinteleLimbiiRomane.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/CuvinteleLimbiiRomane.svg/450px-CuvinteleLimbiiRomane.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/CuvinteleLimbiiRomane.svg/600px-CuvinteleLimbiiRomane.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CuvinteleLimbiiRomane.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Romanian's core lexicon (2,581 words); Marius Sala,
         <i>
          VRLR
         </i>
         (1988)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A statistical analysis sorting Romanian words by etymological source carried out by Macrea (1961)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       based on the DLRM
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (49,649 words) showed the following makeup:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        43% recent Romance loans (mainly French: 38.42%, Latin: 2.39%, Italian: 1.72%)
       </li>
       <li>
        20% inherited Latin
       </li>
       <li>
        11.5% Slavic (
        <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">
         Old Church Slavonic
        </a>
        : 7.98%, Bulgarian: 1.78%, Bulgarian-Serbian: 1.51%)
       </li>
       <li>
        3.62% Turkish
       </li>
       <li>
        2.40% Modern Greek
       </li>
       <li>
        2.17% Hungarian
       </li>
       <li>
        1.77% German
       </li>
       <li>
        8.31% Unknown origin
       </li>
       <li>
        2.24% Onomatopoeic
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       If the analysis is restricted to a core vocabulary of 2,500 frequent, semantically rich and productive words, then the Latin inheritance comes first, followed by Romance and classical Latin neologisms, whereas the Slavic borrowings come third. The Romanian lexicon is similar by 77% with Italian, 75% with French, 74% with
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">
        Sardinian
       </a>
       , 73% with
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       , 72% with Portuguese and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rheto-Romance" title="Rheto-Romance">
        Rheto-Romance
       </a>
       , 71% with Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="barbox" style="overflow-x: auto;">
       <div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:250px; background: white;">
        <table style="border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">

          </td>

            Germanic
           </a>
           (German-based influence, English loanwords)
          </td>
          <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
           <div style="background:gold; width:2.54%; overflow: hidden;">
           </div>
          </td>
          <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">
           2.54%
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">
           <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">
            Greek
           </a>
          </td>
          <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
           <div style="background:darkgreen; width:1.7%; overflow: hidden;">
           </div>
          </td>

           1.7%
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">

          </td>
          <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">

           </div>

           5.49%
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_grammar" title="Romanian grammar">
        Romanian grammar
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Overall Romanian grammar is more conservative in its preservation of Latin grammatical constructs than other Romance languages. (Scholars disagree on the reasons for this).
      </p>
      <p>
       Romanian nouns are characterized by gender (feminine, masculine, and neuter), and
       <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">
        declined
       </a>
       by number (singular and plural) and case (
       <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">
        nominative
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">
        accusative
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">
        dative
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">
        genitive
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vocative_case" title="Vocative case">
        vocative
       </a>
       ). The articles, as well as most adjectives and pronouns,
       <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">
        agree
       </a>
       in gender, number and case with the noun they modify.
      </p>
      <p>
       Romanian is the only Romance language where
       <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">
        definite articles
       </a>
       are
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enclitic" title="Enclitic">
         enclitic
        </a>
       </i>
       : that is, attached to the end of the noun (as in
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        Scandinavian
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">
        Bulgarian
       </a>
       ), instead of in front (
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proclitic" title="Proclitic">
         proclitic
        </a>
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They were formed, as in other Romance languages, from the Latin demonstrative pronouns.
      </p>
      <p>
       As in all Romance languages, Romanian verbs are highly inflected for person, number, tense, mood, and voice. The usual word order in sentences is
       <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93verb%E2%80%93object" title="Subject–verb–object">
        subject–verb–object
       </a>
       (SVO). Romanian has four verbal
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">
        conjugations
       </a>
       which further split into ten conjugation patterns. Verbs can be put in five
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mood_(linguistics)" title="Mood (linguistics)">
        moods
       </a>
       that are inflected for the person (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indicative_mood" title="Indicative mood">
        indicative
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Conditional_mood" title="Conditional mood">
        conditional
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Optative_mood" title="Optative mood">
        optative
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">
        imperative
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">
        subjunctive
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presumptive_mood" title="Presumptive mood">
        presumptive
       </a>
       ) and four impersonal moods (
       <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">
        infinitive
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gerund" title="Gerund">
        gerund
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Supine" title="Supine">
        supine
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">
        participle
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_phonology" title="Romanian phonology">
        Romanian phonology
       </a>
      </div>
      <table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          <img alt="[]" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="75" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/30px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/45px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/60px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 2x" width="30"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
           IPA
          </a>
          phonetic symbols.
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         instead of
         <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
          Unicode
         </a>
         characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          Help:IPA
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Romanian has seven
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
        vowels
       </a>
       :
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɨ/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /u/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ə/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/
       </span>
       . Additionally,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ø/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /y/
       </span>
       may appear in some
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        borrowed words
       </a>
       . Arguably, the diphthongs
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e̯a/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o̯a/
       </span>
       are also part of the phoneme set. There are twenty-two consonants. The two
       <a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">
        approximants
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /j/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /w/
       </span>
       can appear before or after any vowel, creating a large number of glide-vowel sequences which are, strictly speaking, not
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthongs
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In final positions after consonants, a short
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       can be deleted, surfacing only as the
       <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">
        palatalization
       </a>
       of the preceding consonant (e.g.,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [mʲ]
       </span>
       ). Similarly, a deleted
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /u/
       </span>
       may prompt
       <a href="/wiki/Labialization" title="Labialization">
        labialization
       </a>
       of a preceding consonant, though this has ceased to carry any morphological meaning.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetic_changes">
        Phonetic changes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Phonetic changes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin-to-Romanian_sound_changes" title="Latin-to-Romanian sound changes">
        Latin-to-Romanian sound changes
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Owing to its isolation from the other Romance languages, the phonetic evolution of Romanian was quite different, but the language does share a few changes with Italian, such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kl]
       </span>
       →
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kj]
       </span>
       (Lat.
       <b>
        cl
       </b>
       arus → Rom.
       <b>
        chi
       </b>
       ar, Ital.
       <b>
        chi
       </b>
       aro, Lat. clamare → Rom.
       <i>
        che
       </i>
       mare, Ital.
       <i>
        chi
       </i>
       amare) and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɡl]
       </span>
       →
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɡj]
       </span>
       (Lat. *
       <i>
        gl
       </i>
       acia (
       <i>
        gl
       </i>
       acies) → Rom.
       <i>
        ghe
       </i>
       ață, Ital.
       <i>
        ghi
       </i>
       accia,
       <i>
        ghi
       </i>
       accio, Lat. *un
       <i>
        gl
       </i>
       a (ungula) → Rom. un
       <i>
        ghi
       </i>
       e, Ital. un
       <i>
        ghi
       </i>
       a), although this did not go as far as it did in Italian with other similar clusters (Rom.
       <i>
        pl
       </i>
       ace, Ital.
       <i>
        pi
       </i>
       ace); another similarity with Italian is the change from
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ke]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ki]
       </span>
       to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tʃe]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tʃi]
       </span>
       (Lat. pax, pa
       <i>
        ce
       </i>
       m → Rom. and Ital. pa
       <i>
        ce
       </i>
       , Lat. dul
       <i>
        ce
       </i>
       m → Rom. dul
       <i>
        ce
       </i>
       , Ital. dol
       <i>
        ce
       </i>
       , Lat.
       <i>
        ci
       </i>
       rcus → Rom.
       <i>
        ce
       </i>
       rc, Ital.
       <i>
        ci
       </i>
       rco) and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɡe]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɡi]
       </span>
       to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dʒe]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dʒi]
       </span>
       (Lat.
       <i>
        ge
       </i>
       lu → Rom.
       <i>
        ge
       </i>
       r, Ital.
       <i>
        ge
       </i>
       lo, Lat. mar
       <i>
        gi
       </i>
       nem → Rom. and Ital. mar
       <i>
        gi
       </i>
       ne, Lat.
       <i>
        ge
       </i>
       mere → Rom.
       <i>
        ge
       </i>
       me (
       <i>
        ge
       </i>
       mere), Ital.
       <i>
        ge
       </i>
       mere). There are also a few changes shared with
       <a href="/wiki/Dalmatian_language" title="Dalmatian language">
        Dalmatian
       </a>
       , such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɡn/
       </span>
       (probably phonetically
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ŋn]
       </span>
       ) →
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [mn]
       </span>
       (Lat. co
       <b>
        gn
       </b>
       atus → Rom. cu
       <b>
        mn
       </b>
       at, Dalm. co
       <b>
        mn
       </b>
       ut) and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ks/
       </span>
       →
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ps]
       </span>
       in some situations (Lat. coxa → Rom. coa
       <i>
        ps
       </i>
       ă, Dalm. co
       <i>
        ps
       </i>
       a).
      </p>
      <p>
       Among the notable phonetic changes are:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        diphthongization of e and o → ea and oa, before ă (or e as well, in the case of o) in the next syllable:
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Lat. c
          <b>
           e
          </b>
          ra → Rom. c
          <b>
           ea
          </b>
          ră (wax)
         </li>
         <li>
          Lat. s
          <b>
           o
          </b>
          le → Rom. s
          <b>
           oa
          </b>
          re (sun)
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Iotation" title="Iotation">
         iotation
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [e]
        </span>
        →
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ie]
        </span>
        in the beginning of the word
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Lat. h
          <b>
           e
          </b>
          rba → Rom.
          <b>
           ia
          </b>
          rbă (grass, herb)
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        velar
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [k ɡ]
        </span>
        → labial
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [p b m]
        </span>
        before alveolar consonants and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [w]
        </span>
        (e.g.
        <b>
         ngu
        </b>
        →
        <b>
         mb
        </b>
        ):
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Lat. o
          <b>
           ct
          </b>
          o → Rom. o
          <b>
           pt
          </b>
          (eight)
         </li>
         <li>
          Lat. li
          <b>
           ng
          </b>
          ua → Rom. li
          <b>
           mb
          </b>
          ă (tongue, language)
         </li>
         <li>
          Lat. si
          <b>
           gn
          </b>
          um → Rom. se
          <b>
           mn
          </b>
          (sign)
         </li>
         <li>
          Lat. co
          <b>
           x
          </b>
          a → Rom. coa
          <b>
           ps
          </b>
          ă (thigh)
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Rhotacism_(sound_change)" title="Rhotacism (sound change)">
         rhotacism
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [l]
        </span>
        →
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [r]
        </span>
        between vowels
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Lat. cae
          <b>
           l
          </b>
          um → Rom. ce
          <b>
           r
          </b>
          (sky)
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Alveolars
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [d t]
        </span>
        palatalized to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [(d)z] [ts]
        </span>
        when before short
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [e]
        </span>
        or long
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [iː]
        </span>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Lat.
          <b>
           d
          </b>
          eus → Rom.
          <b>
           z
          </b>
          eu (god)
         </li>
         <li>
          Lat.
          <b>
           t
          </b>
          enem → Rom.
          <b>
           ț
          </b>
          ine (hold)
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       On the other hand, Romanian (along with French) has
       <i>
        lost
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /kw/
       </span>
       (
       <b>
        qu
       </b>
       ) sound before
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/
       </span>
       from original Latin, turning it either into
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /p/
       </span>
       (Lat.
       <b>
        qu
       </b>
       attuor → Rom.
       <i>
        patru
       </i>
       , "four"; cf. It.
       <i>
        quattro
       </i>
       ) or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /k/
       </span>
       (Lat.
       <b>
        qu
       </b>
       ando → Rom.
       <i>
        când
       </i>
       , "when"; Lat.
       <b>
        qu
       </b>
       ale → Rom.
       <i>
        care
       </i>
       , "which").
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">
        Writing system
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Writing system">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scrisoarea-lui-neacsu-din-campulung.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1500" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Scrisoarea-lui-neacsu-din-campulung.jpg/220px-Scrisoarea-lui-neacsu-din-campulung.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Scrisoarea-lui-neacsu-din-campulung.jpg/330px-Scrisoarea-lui-neacsu-din-campulung.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Scrisoarea-lui-neacsu-din-campulung.jpg/440px-Scrisoarea-lui-neacsu-din-campulung.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scrisoarea-lui-neacsu-din-campulung.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Neac%C8%99u%27s_letter" title="Neacșu's letter">
          Neacșu's letter
         </a>
         is the oldest surviving document written in Romanian
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The first written record about a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">
        Romance language
       </a>
       spoken in the Middle Ages in the Balkans is from 587. A Vlach muleteer accompanying the Byzantine army noticed that the load was falling from one of the animals and shouted to a companion
       <i>
        Torna, torna frate
       </i>
       (meaning "Return, return brother!"), and, "sculca" (out of bed) .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theophanes_Confessor" title="Theophanes Confessor">
        Theophanes Confessor
       </a>
       recorded it as part of a 6th-century military expedition by
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Commentiolus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Commentiolus (page does not exist)">
        Commentiolus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Priscus" title="Priscus">
        Priscus
       </a>
       against the Avars and Slovenes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%22Libri_III_de_moribus_et_actis_primorum_Normanniae_ducum%22&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title='"Libri III de moribus et actis primorum Normanniae ducum" (page does not exist)'>
        "Libri III de moribus et actis primorum Normanniae ducum"
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Dudo_of_Saint-Quentin" title="Dudo of Saint-Quentin">
        Dudo of Saint-Quentin
       </a>
       states that
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_Normandy" title="Richard I of Normandy">
        Richard I of Normandy
       </a>
       was sent by his father
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_I_Longsword" title="William I Longsword">
        William I Longsword
       </a>
       to learn the
       <a href="/wiki/Dacian_language" title="Dacian language">
        Dacian language
       </a>
       with
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bothon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bothon (page does not exist)">
        Bothon
       </a>
       because the inhabitants of
       <a href="/wiki/Bayeux" title="Bayeux">
        Bayeux
       </a>
       spoke more Dacian than
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Roman
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The oldest surviving written text in Romanian is a letter from late June 1521,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in which Neacșu of
       <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A2mpulung" title="Câmpulung">
        Câmpulung
       </a>
       wrote to the mayor of
       <a href="/wiki/Bra%C8%99ov" title="Brașov">
        Brașov
       </a>
       about an imminent attack of the Turks. It was written using the
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Romanian Cyrillic alphabet">
        Cyrillic alphabet
       </a>
       , like most early Romanian writings. The earliest surviving writing in Latin script was a late 16th-century
       <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">
        Transylvanian
       </a>
       text which was written with the
       <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_alphabet" title="Hungarian alphabet">
        Hungarian alphabet
       </a>
       conventions.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Romanian-kirilitza-tatal-nostru.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="274" data-file-width="958" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Romanian-kirilitza-tatal-nostru.jpg/300px-Romanian-kirilitza-tatal-nostru.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Romanian-kirilitza-tatal-nostru.jpg/450px-Romanian-kirilitza-tatal-nostru.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Romanian-kirilitza-tatal-nostru.jpg/600px-Romanian-kirilitza-tatal-nostru.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Romanian-kirilitza-tatal-nostru.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A sample of Romanian written in the
         <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Romanian Cyrillic alphabet">
          Romanian Cyrillic alphabet
         </a>
         , which was still in use in the early 19th century
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the late 18th century,
       <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">
        Transylvanian
       </a>
       scholars noted the Latin origin of Romanian and adapted the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
        Latin alphabet
       </a>
       to the Romanian language, using some orthographic rules from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_alphabet" title="Italian alphabet">
        Italian
       </a>
       , recognized as Romanian's closest relative. The Cyrillic alphabet remained in (gradually decreasing) use until 1860, when Romanian writing was first officially regulated.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moldavian_SSR" title="Moldavian SSR">
        Soviet Republic of Moldova
       </a>
       , a special version of the Cyrillic alphabet derived from the Russian version was used until 1989, when Romanian language spoken there officially returned to the Romanian Latin alphabet, although in the breakaway territory of Transnistria the Cyrillic alphabet is used to this day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Romanian_alphabet">
        Romanian alphabet
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Romanian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Romanian alphabet">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_alphabet" title="Romanian alphabet">
        Romanian alphabet
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanian_braille" title="Romanian braille">
        Romanian braille
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Romanian alphabet is as follows:
      </p>
      <center>
       <dl>
        <dd>
         <table class="wikitable" style="border-collapse:collapse">
          <tr>
           <td colspan="31" style="background:#efefef; text-align:center;">

            </b>
           </td>
          </tr>
          <tr style="text-align:center;">
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/A" title="A">
             A
            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/%C4%82" title="Ă">
             Ă
            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/%C3%82" title="Â">
             Â
            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/B" title="B">
             B
            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/C" title="C">
             C
            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/D" title="D">

            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/E" title="E">

            </a>
           </td>

            </a>
           </td>
           <td>

           </td>

            <a href="/wiki/%C3%8E" title="Î">

            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/J" title="J">

            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/K" title="K">
             K
            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/L" title="L">

            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/M" title="M">

            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/N" title="N">
             N
            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            <a href="/wiki/O" title="O">
             O
            </a>
           </td>
